Free Talk

Reader's Required Understanding

— Gao Tang

Those who are literate all want to read, but what to read is the question. Some people’s attitude toward reading is: only new and good books are worth reading, even limiting themselves to books on political theory. Therefore, those living in big cities suffer from having too many books, both good and bad, old and new, fearing to read a bad book and thus not knowing what to read. Those living in remote, poor villages suffer from the lack of new books, and the ones available are not worth reading. At school, some feel what they learn cannot be applied, and they only cope with homework, seeking extracurricular books to read—this is one type. Another type specializes in one subject, not reading anything unrelated (as they deem it unrelated). Thus, some doing technical work or engaged in artistic work only read a few books they need, and ignore everything else.

Both of these attitudes have shortcomings. Seeking new and good books to read is correct, because reading is for increasing knowledge, and reading new and good books enables us to learn good and useful knowledge. But old books are not completely devoid of good things. For example, old novels describing the current state of society, such as *Water Margin*, *The Scholars*, *The Bureaucrats*, etc., can enrich our social knowledge. And researching Chinese history inevitably requires using many old historical books as materials. These kinds of books are not only available in big cities, but can also be found in remote, poor villages. But this is not to say that everyone should go and read old books; it's just saying that besides reading new books, old books also have their value. Things are different at school. School textbooks may not all be good, but there are indeed many things that must be learned, such as mathematics, physics, chemistry, engineering, history, geography, accounting, etc. These are all things that can be learned well in school. As for foreign languages, it goes without saying. In culturally backward China, reading materials often cannot satisfy our needs. Therefore, learning a foreign language well means acquiring a powerful tool for reading. Should extracurricular books be read? Of course, they should, but one cannot completely ignore one's schoolwork while reading extracurricular books. Secondly, we should also know that new and good books are not available everywhere, and even if they are available, we may not be able to read whatever we want due to various reasons. Therefore, I believe both new and old books can be read.

Furthermore, specializing in one subject is the right thing to do. Not only that, but we also advocate "learn what you do." Because if I do technical work and cannot do it well or master the technology, I will never be proficient. Engaging in artistic work without understanding art is even worse. But we cannot therefore put up a sign saying "Don't talk about national affairs" and not read other books. No matter what subject we study, we must have a general basic knowledge. Moreover, no matter what we learn, it is to serve society. If we don't understand what kind of society the current society is, even if we shout about service a lot, it's just empty talk. Therefore, in addition to specializing in one subject, we must also study other books.

In this way, I think at least the following books are worth reading: (1) Various progressive theoretical books, (2) Natural sciences, (3) History and geography, (4) Old novels, ancient books. In addition, novels, dramas, music, and various other books that are beneficial to personal improvement, the masses, and cultural cultivation can all be read. And in order to increase reading tools, learning foreign languages is also necessary. But no matter what books one reads or what subjects one studies, one must strive for depth in a certain area. If one reads every book and studies every problem without seeking deep understanding (in fact, one cannot achieve deep understanding in this way), one will become a jack-of-all-trades, master of none. Knowing a little about everything, but not truly understanding anything, is like being skilled in all eighteen martial arts, but proficient in none.

Some people put a lot of effort into reading, even being able to recite the sentences in a book, but they cannot apply them. There are also people who read superficially, skimming through without seeking thorough understanding. After reading, they can only say that they have read such a book, but have not gained anything from it. There are also people who do not distinguish between right and wrong when reading books with completely opposing views, seeking correct opinions. This book is right, that book is also right, and ultimately they don't know who is right, becoming even more confused after reading. There are even people who are satisfied with having a bookshelf full of progressive books, flipping through them when they are happy, and letting insects eat them when they are not. This is not reading, but using books as decorations. In fact, such decorations are not only useless, but sometimes harmful. Reading in this way not only fails to gain useful things from bad books, but also fails to gain useful things from good books. Therefore, studying the method of reading—how to read—becomes necessary. (Payment of Poverty Relief Money)

Round and Square

— Ke Wei

Round and square are two different adjectives, one is multi-faceted, the other is round and smooth.

Whenever we talk about someone who is serious and inflexible in doing things, we call him "walking square steps"; for someone who is not subdued by power and wealth, we call his personality "upright." At the same time, for someone who can adapt to the environment on all sides and please everyone, we call him "slick," like a ball that is in equilibrium in physics and can survive in any environment.

On the surface, a slick person seems to always get the advantage. He can adapt to the environment and get praise from all sides. Slickness is indeed a good adjective, but it should be used in moderation, otherwise it will become sycophancy.

An upright person is often cursed as a "stubborn head," but his loyalty, seriousness, and integrity are his virtues. The world's great men and scientists have this spirit of serious work to achieve ultimate success.

Scenes from the Countryside

Self-Respect and Self-Boasting

— Jie Ren

If a person wants to achieve some success in life, he must have self-respect—respect for his own personality, thoughts, actions, and responsibilities. This is also a mentality that everyone should have.

With self-respect, humanity has new developments, and society can move forward. Conversely, if humanity does not have this kind of self-respecting and competitive spirit, then humanity does not know its own value and its own position, and indeed does not trust its own strength, not believing that its own strength can achieve great undertakings. Therefore, it loses its enterprising spirit and cannot seek the development of life, and society will never have the hope of moving forward. So self-respect is the most reliable capital in life. It is more effective than money, power, and any help from relatives and friends. It can enable people to overcome difficulties and obstacles, and achieve success in their careers.

However, a self-boasting person is not necessarily a self-respecting person, nor does he necessarily have an enterprising spirit. Nowadays, in society, there are self-boasting and arrogant people everywhere. They will describe their family's small assets as vast, and how lavishly they can act in social situations, and how rich their lives are. Many people can be deceived by him, and he can become even more successful. But once the truth is exposed, the result he gets is only everyone's contempt, and he can never be trusted again.

Exaggerating oneself not only fails to make others respect you, but sometimes even if you have very good achievements, the result of self-boasting can make them insignificant. A great person never boasts. Once he boasts, no matter how great he is, he will become small. A self-boasting person is definitely not a self-respecting person. Only those who can respect themselves can be respected by others. Loyal friends! A person without self-respect will never leave deep traces on the road to success. Let us never boast! Let us uphold our self-respect! No matter what kind of undertaking, we will never fail!

Teacher for All Ages

— Written by James Hilton, Translated by Yi Jin

(12)

Then he mentioned that he had been at Brookfield for forty-two years, and he was very happy there. "This has been my life," he said simply. "O miki praeteritos refora i i jupiter annos... uh hum—I don't have to—of course—translate it." Much laughter.

I remember many changes at Brookfield. I remember—uh hum—the first bicycle. I remember when there were no gas lamps or electric lights. In our administration department, we always used a person called a lamp-lighter—he did nothing else but wipe, trim, and light all the lamps in the school. I remember that winter term when the ground was a sheet of ice, and it refused to thaw for seven weeks—there were no other games, and the whole school was learning to skate on the swamp. That was probably around 1880. I remember when two-thirds of the school had German measles, and the hall was turned into a hospital ward. I remember our grand fireworks display on Victory Night. It was placed too close to the big awning, and we had to call the fire department to put it out. But the firefighters were also holding their own celebrations, and most of them—uh hum—were also getting into quite an embarrassing state. (Laughter) I remember Mrs. Brool, whose picture still hangs in the snack shop. She worked there until an uncle in Australia left her some money and she stopped. Indeed, I remember a lot, and so I often think I should write a book. What should I call it now? "Memoirs of a Cane and Chalk," how about it? (Cheers and laughter. This was a good joke, everyone thought—one of Chips's best.) Well, well, someday I will write it. But I still like to tell you about it. Really. I remember... I remember... but mainly I remember the faces of each of you. I will never forget them. In my mind, there are thousands of faces—the faces of children. If you come to see me again in a few years—I hope you all do—I will remember those old faces of yours, but perhaps it is very likely that I will not remember—and then one day you will see me somewhere else, and I still won't recognize you, and you will say to yourself, "This old fellow doesn't remember me." (32)

A Practitioner of Wealth Preservation

— Qi Ji

Since this newspaper published Mr. Nie Yuntai's method of wealth preservation, it has been widely circulated and recited, touching the hearts of many people. However, it is not yet known how many wealthy people can put it into practice, because it is easy to talk about, but when it comes to practice, there are bound to be difficulties. As the saying goes, knowing is not difficult, but doing is. We don't need to talk about this, and we will see the results in the future. What I want to introduce to readers now is a practitioner of wealth preservation from the Ming Dynasty, whose behavior is worth studying.

In Zhang Dafu's *Meihua Caotang Ji* [Collected Writings from the Plum Blossom Cottage] there is such a record:

"A eunuch from Western Shu [Sichuan] served as an investigating censor. He had five sons, and he set up middle and lower-class properties for each of them, only giving them porridge and gruel. He himself wore and used only what was necessary, never allowing any surplus. When he was old and long-lived, he took out the accumulated emoluments he had saved over his lifetime, which amounted to ten thousand taels of silver, and wished to donate them to supplement the public treasury. The officials reported that the public treasury was not in deficit and that there were no public affairs to which to contribute. The eunuch then requested that he be allowed to dig a hole in an abandoned courtyard and bury the money, inscribing on a stone tablet: 'Returning to the Creator.' After a hundred years, the cellar remained as before. In the *Xin You* year of the *Wanli* reign [1621], the bandit chief created chaos, and public and private property in Chengdu Prefecture was almost completely looted. The scholars and people of Chengdu Prefecture had nothing to eat and were on the verge of collapse. Someone who knew about this matter reported it to the officials, and they were able to avoid complete ruin. This old man's noble righteousness pierces through the ages. Regardless, the eunuchs who served in this land, and the scholars and people of this land, were all honest officials and upright men."

Readers may laugh at this gentleman for being too foolish, and asking why he had to bury the money. My point is that perhaps there were no charitable organizations at that time, and he originally wanted to donate it to the government, but the government did not accept it. At this point, another person might have changed his original intention and still divided the property among his children. Others could not say that he was going back on his word, because there was really nowhere to put the money. But this gentleman regarded this money as if it were poisonous snakes and fierce beasts; no matter what, he would not allow it to remain in the hands of his children, and would rather request to dig a hole in an abandoned courtyard and bury it, rather than bequeath a hundred generations of misfortune to his descendants. When we see this, we can't help but admire his foresight. It is really beyond the reach of ordinary wealthy people. His understanding of the principles of wealth preservation was so thorough that he knew everything about it, not just a little superficial knowledge. Therefore, once he made up his mind, he would never change it. When we read about wealth preservation, we should also carefully consider the truth it tells. If we don't understand it very well after reading it once, we may as well read it several times until we fully understand it, so that when we put it into practice, there will be no hesitation.

After Reading "Korean Spring"

— Xiao Shuming

In the long-silent Shanghai literary world, I recently read a literary work that is quite endearing, namely "Korean Spring" by the Korean writer Zhang Hezhu.

There are so many similarities between the Korean and Chinese peoples. Therefore, in Korean literature, we often find our own shadows, and sometimes we find our own character even more. Of course, these shadows and characters are somewhat different in terms of the era, that is, the character of Koreans in this period may be found in the character of Chinese in the previous period, but we cannot deny that in many parts of China today, we can still see the characters of many people in "Korean Spring."

"Korean Spring" is a landscape painting drawn with a painter's brush, and also a recitation poem accompanied by moving music. From the descriptions of the author Zhang Hezhu, we see the flowers and plants of Korea, the people of Korea, the mountains and rivers of Korea, the villages of Korea, and even the architecture of the cities. There are many landscapes and plants that can evoke our emotions, and many lonely people and events that can arouse our sorrow. And the author's method of description, as the translator Fan Quan said in the "Preface," is to use "a fine steel needle to gently prick the skin of people," so its "hidden pain penetrates deep into people's hearts." But the author does not only make people feel "hidden pain," and sometimes uses a passionate tone to speak for the future of Korea. For example, at the end of "Korean Spring," he says, "Although the fields and mountain peaks are coming to spring, it is not known when the spring of humanity will come. Can spring only be in flowers and no longer have a time when 'spring is in humanity'?" At the end of "Beautiful Korea," he says again: "The nature of Korea should be beautiful, and in fact there are many beautiful places. If people can have a prosperous life, then I think it will definitely be even more beautiful." So he finally shouts: "So I eagerly look forward to the early appearance of such a Korea!"

Therefore, for so many years, the translator's criticism of the author is correct: "He gave candor, sincerity, and passion to the readers, and with a burning pen, powerfully implied the future spring of this Korea." (See "Preface")

It is also this candor, sincerity, and passion that makes us—Chinese readers—feel infinitely kind! Reading it, it feels as if the author's words are our own, and we feel that what the author says is what we want to say.

In the long-silent Shanghai literary world, the publication of such a vibrant literary work that makes Chinese readers feel close is a fortunate achievement worth celebrating. So here, I have written this short essay to express my praise for this meaningful work with a little enthusiasm.

Social Services

Elementary School Teachers' Medical Aid Fund—Hospital Doctors Start to Sponsor

The "Elementary School Teachers' Medical Aid Fund" initiated by this newspaper has received enthusiastic responses from all parties. "Those with money contribute money, and those with strength contribute strength." It has gained widespread sympathy from people in society, which is precious and touching! Yesterday, we received a letter from Dr. Song Guobin, former president of the Chinese Medical Association, who generously donated 500 yuan of New Year's entertainment expenses to the medical aid fund, and attached the following simple rules for preferential treatment of elementary school teachers for the diagnosis and treatment of lung diseases: (1) Elementary school teachers must present proof of identity from their school when seeking medical treatment. (2) Registration fees, consultation fees, and cold light test fees are all completely waived. (3) Those who are confirmed to have lung disease after the test can enjoy a 50% discount on cold light treatment fees. (4) Test time: 4:00 PM to 6:00 PM. (5) Address: Dr. Song's clinic at 1193 Route Lafayette.

Preferential Treatment Measures at the Special District Hospital

The Municipal Special District Hospital also responded with a letter. Stipulations for preferential treatment are as per Article 10, and 300 medical treatment certificates are attached, requesting distribution to impoverished teachers in various schools for medical treatment. The preferential treatment measures are as follows: (1) Free diagnosis and treatment of diseases. (2) Free physical examinations. (3) Free vaccinations. (4) Reduced surgical fees. (5) Medicines supplied at cost (also provided free of charge to the poor). (6) Reduced hospitalization fees. (7) Reduced testing fees. (8) Reduced electrotherapy fees. (9) Reduced delivery fees. (10) Obligatory guidance of general health knowledge. The hospital is located at the intersection of 1000 Xiaoshadu Road and Penang Road and is open on Sundays as usual.

Minsheng Pharmaceutical Factory Donates 1,000 Yuan Worth of Medicine

Kangjian Dental Clinic at 63 Route Mercier, French Concession, sent a letter stating that from the 10th of this month, from 9:00 AM to 11:00 AM, elementary school teachers who bring a certificate for examination will be exempted from the registration fee, and the medical fee will be halved. The Shanghai Chinese Hospital Department Hospital at 32 Huade Road, Tilanqiao, and the branch hospital at 251 Xinzha Road, have also formulated free outpatient and hospitalization measures. In addition, the Shanghai distribution office of Minsheng Pharmaceutical Factory sent a letter generously donating 1,000 yuan worth of medicines. All of these benevolent and righteous acts are truly inspiring.

Free Schools Directory

Public Works Bureau Night School: The Public Works Bureau People's Night School at 1461 Xinzha Road recruits young people aged 14 to 20, with a level equivalent to elementary school to junior high school. The tuition fee is 5 yuan per semester, and books and supplies are provided by the school. Classes are held daily from 6:30 PM to 8:30 PM, and the school is scheduled to open on the 13th.

Huinan Elementary School: Thanks to the enthusiastic sponsorship of School Director Wang Shengchang, a special donation of 20 free places and 40 half-fee places, books are provided by themselves. All impoverished and outstanding children are welcome to apply.

Dongguang Elementary School: In addition to the five free places already funded by Mr. Wang Peiyuan, Dongguang Elementary School on Yangshupu Road also has ten half-fee places and five free places. All impoverished and outstanding children can apply.

Gulin Elementary School: The Gulin Elementary School in the Gongyi Workshop on Route Robert, founded by Zhu Shunlu and presided over by Chen Jiping, was originally intended to help out-of-school children in western Shanghai. The fees are extremely low. Now, in order to respond to this newspaper's educational aid efforts, 100 additional free places have been added. In addition to charging only 15 yuan for books and 10 yuan for miscellaneous expenses, all other expenses are waived.

Amateur Library

The Chinese Amateur Library on the 7th floor of Pudong Building has a collection of more than 20,000 volumes and more than 2,000 readers. Established three years ago, it has lent more than 150,000 books of various types. Recently, a board of directors was formed, and Zhang Yipeng and Gui Yanfang were elected as chairman and vice-chairman, respectively. Chen Qingshi, Lu Gaoyi, Li Hongshou, and others serve as executive directors, carrying out fundraising, with excellent results. The library's affairs have been greatly refreshed, and there has been an increase in books, newspapers, and magazines. It is now planning to promote a book collection campaign and expand the recruitment of readers. It is said that the procedure for joining the library is very simple. In addition to filling out an application form and paying a deposit of 10 yuan to obtain a reading card, there is only a reading fee of 1 yuan per month. If the family is impoverished and unable to pay the fees, there are still free methods stipulated.
